20 Years Ago— June 12, 1015 
The long standing feud between 
the then Resident Commissioner 
Manuel L. Quezon and Dr. Domf * 
nador Gomez arising from the 
statement made by the Filipino 
ehvoy before the United States 
senare to the effect that Domina- 
dor Gomez had been ousted from 
the Philippine assembly for certain 
reason ended this day. Quezon, 
the papers recorded, was said to 
have offered his r pology to the 
fiery doctor saying that he “was 
very sorry that tiie incident had 
ever occurred.” It was said that 
Gomez sent two envoys in the 
person of ex-Colonel Blanco and 
Jose Varela at the Quezon resi- 
dence in Sampaloc to demand ex- 
planation of the resident commis- 
sioner about his testimony before 
the U. S. senate. Quezon explain- 
ed he was not seeking personal 
benefit by making that statement 
against Gomez. 

High government officials and 
prominent __ businessmen gathered 
around the festive board at the 
Manila Hotel this evening in honor 
of the visiting congressional mis- 
sion: Senator John Shafroth, Con- 
gressmen Clarence R. Miller, Ri- 
chard W. Austin and Clement 
Braumbaugh. Among the speakers 
besides the guests of honor were 
{Resident Commissioner Quezon, 

• Frank B. Ingersoll, former ’ Vice 
Governor General Newton Gilbert, 
C. Nesbitt and Mr. Duffy who was 
the toastmaster. Quezon whoso 
postprandial talk outshone those 
of the rest was introduced as the 
son of a little Baler farmer who 
made good, graduate of San Juan 
de Letran and Sto. Tomas, who 
spoke Spanish with an American 
accent and who was popularly 
called in the American capital as 
/‘Casey,” “We have confidence in 
the purposes of the American peo- 
ple and we are friends of the Am- 
aricans,” M. L. Q, began. “Revolu- 
tions exist only in the columns of 
the newspapers...’’ he continued.;/ 
“We ask congress to decide the 
Philippine question without pre- 
judice and hatred.” Congressman * 
Austin called the Filipinos mostly 
illiterate and as such incapable of 
independence, a statement which 
provoked considerable comment in . 
the press the succeeding days. 

On the world war: 

Russians whipped the Germans 
near Dniester River, captured 17 
German guns, 188 officers and 6- 
500 Austrian and German soldiers. 

An Austrian torpedo boat 

was sunk in Gulf of Tireste by 
Italian submarine.

The Governor said further. “The the Filipinos j n Washington. I saw 

how the administration ha*, impli- 



Roosevelt personally. The Philip, 
pines is most fortunate in having 
him as Governor General now, 
at this most crucial period of 
our history. Our people will 
never be able to repay him 
for the immeasurable service h* 
has rendered their cause. The va- 
lue of his work for us, we shall 
realize and appreciate more in the 
future than now.” 



That’s how our Filipino leader 
salutes Governor Murphy . But. how 
shall we salute our President Que- 
zon? How shall we be able to show 
gratitude to him who has devoted 
the best years of his life to the wel- 
fare of this country and’ the Filipi- 
no people. He whose leadership has 
manned the nation through the 
storms of its political life and Is 
now safely drifting it into harbor? 
Nothing can be a more glowing 
tribute to this man than a con- 
certed effort of the Filipino peo- 
ple to build the Commonwealth on 
a basic foundation of harmony and 
good-will. It is only thus that we 
can offer to President Quezon a liv- 
ing testimonial of our faith in hhh. 
A successful establishment <>; the 
new government is a successful tri- 
bute of the race to the man. who 
has weathered all the ravages of the 
elements and time in order to prove 
his worth to .the land that give’ 
him birth. 

We cannot, o r caught napping 
now. The

ahead of us require a solid front. 

They call for mass nationalism, not 
kind, of patriotism that . the 
safcdalists have been unfortunately 
taught to embrace . They need a 

much deeper sense of responsibility, 
a much heavier sacrifice for the. 
common good. The task awaiting 
us demands prudence in the conso- 
lidation of our efforts and a one* 
ness in the unification of our. inter- 



ests so that the Commonwealth can 
succeed. 

It is, however, very notable that 
despite the past 'insanity of a re- 
calcitrant group, there is a very 
conspicuous union among the mas- 
ses now that they have come to a 
point of awakening. It seems as 
if every , element of our race is bent 
on giving its share towards the 
dawning of a hope-born dream. 

For aside from 1 the nation-wide 
movement of economic protection- 
ism, our university heads our now 
working on a “culture program” to 
prepare the students to pull them- 
selves together should anything 
cron up during the transition pe- 
riod In the case of the state uni- * ^ r V

Orient With the end in view to 
creating possible markets for our 
goods in the Orient, just in 
case. . . 

The inauguration of 'the Com- 
monwealth will usher in a new era. 

It will also test our true concepts 
of a government al! our own. That 
it will mean a lot to us cannot be 
denied. And that we shall want to 
celebrate its coming to the full is 
our just pride. But it Is best that 
we .take a cue from Representative 
Marabut who would not go for a 
lavish demonstration. A modest 
inauguration is surely a sensible 
thing to do. The fact that the 
inaugural ceremonies will be wit- 
nessed by a large delegation of dis- 
tinguished visitor?? from abroad 
should not let our vanity steal the 
better of our prudence. The new 
government should be ushered in a 
manner compatible to our national 
capacity. To hold, simple inaugu- 
ral ceremonies for the Common- 
wealth will in sr*0~ way / reject 
. against our integrity. Rather’ it 
Will reveal us in pur characteristic 
modesty and simplicity, a national- 
trait which ought to be injected 
^wholesomely into the Common- 
wealth

Common Sense 

A Filipino’s View 
By JUAN F. IilLAR 10 




/«■- 36 



PRANK MURPHY— THE MAX 



Governor General Frank Mur- 
l>hy has returned. 

Graciously he declined the 
honors justly deserved. But he 
i ailed in his good intentions, be- 
cause the Filipino people knew 
how faithfully and ably lie lias 
served them during his visit in 
the United States. 

Even Senate Presi dent Manue l 
( ^ oes n °t feel asopt inus- 
tic about the coming Philippine 
Commonwealth. 

In serving the Filipino people 
while lie was on an official visit' 
in the United States, Governor 
General Frank Murphy overwork- 
ed himself to such an extent that 
the operation on his tonsils be- 
came more serious than it would 
have been had he not dedicated 
himself without let-up to the cause 
of the Filipino people. 

Because of the implicit con fid- j 
once of President Franklin D. 
Roosevelt in him, all the problems ; 
of the coming government have * 
been solved and or are in the ; 
course of solution in so far as they 
depend upon the action of the Ad- ; 
ministration at Washington, D. C. 

His conferences with the inter- 
departmental committee revealed 
the fact that Governor General j 
Frank Murphy is the one Amer-j 
ican today who has studied and 
msatered the details and impli- 
cations of a change of government. 

The splendid thing about his 
labors in the United States was 
that they were done quietly but 
very effectively. 

The story of all his achievements • 
is yet to be .told, but I am afraid 
that it will not be adequately 
told at this time, because of his in- 
nate sense of medesty. 

I believe that Senate President 



Quezon has told briefly about Gov- 
ernor General Murphy's work in 
the United States. 

In defending and safeguarding 
Filipino welfare and interests, he 
did it in such splendid manner that 
he did not antagonize certain 
American interests whose hostility 
toward us has been open and un- 
equivocal during the past decade. 

Ho has overcome practically all 
the obstacles. It now remains 
for the Philippine Commonwealth 
to preserve whatever lias been won. 
But I have serious doubts about 
it. 

It is now a certainty that Gover- 
nor General Frank Murphy lias 
returned to be the last American 
Chief Executive and to be the first 
American High Commissioner. 

In my opinion, Governor Gen- 
eral Frank Murphy stands out 
conspicuously in the history of co- 
lonial administration. 

The Filipino people owe there- 
fore an eternal debt of gratitude 
to him which in the words of Sen- 
ate President Quezon, "our people 
will never be able to repay him 
for the immesurable service he lias 
rendered their cause." 

With all due respect and without 
any intention of being irreverent, 
I will make a contrast. 

If General Hugh S. Johnson <*f 
NRA fame has been called the 
professional "bad boy" of Amer- 
ican life, our humble Chief Execu- 
tive may be aptly* called the "good 
boy" despite the fact that certain 
vested interests in Detroit have 
branded him as a "radical." To 
be a radical is not bad per se. 

The "radical" former Mayor < f 
Detroit is about to be the darner 
of the "radicals" of this country 
without force and bloodshed.

rence with Senate President Ma-

Justice Jose Yulo. The secretary 
advised the chief executive on the 
legal features of the proclama- 
tion. 

Governor Murphy and Senate 
President Quezon also discussed at 
length the program of legislation 
to be followed by the legislature 
with a view to facilitating the es- 
tablishment of the new govern- 
ment likewise, they agreed on 
several measures designed to in- 
sure peace and social justice in 
the Islands during the life of the 



Commonwealth. 

Both the chief executive and the 



senate head expressed concern 
over the situation of the common 
people. They agreed that reme- 
dial legislation should be adopted 
to safeguard the welfare and inte- 
a^st of the common people. The 
senate head informed the Gover- 
nor General that he will urge the 
legislature to enact labor-social 
measures that will do away with 
social unrest in the future. 

Likewise, the two officials dis- 
cussed the problems in connection 
with the coming general elections 
especially the expenses to be in- 
curred by the insular government 
President Quezon anticipated that 
the coming election will be peace- 
ful as all necessary steps to in- 
j sure peace will be taken by the 
! government.

Quezon To Give Delegates Free Hand; 
Nomination Of Candidates 
Will Take Place 



»«\Mr 

' 3 ' 



The formal nomination of the candidates of the 
majority party 'for president and vice-president of the com- 
monwealth, and the expected approval of the coalition 
ticket of president Quezon and Senator Osmena will be the 
principal items in the program of the majority convention 
next Sunday at the Rizal tennis stadium, according to a 
draft of the complete program for the convention released 
today. 



In view of the known attitude of 
the majority in favor of the coali- 
tion, it is taken for granted that 
Senate-President Quezon will be 
nominated for president and Sen- 
ator Sergio Osmena, head of the 
minority party, will be supported 
for vice-president. 

However, by instruction of Sen- 
ate-President Quezon himself, the 
delegates at the convention will be 
given an absolutely free hand in 
deciding on the coalition and on 
the persons to bo nominated can- 
didates. 

The complete program of the 
convention follows: 

! 1 . Opening of the convention by 

the president of the party. 

2. Reading of list of delegates; 



declaration of a quorum. 

3. Appointment of credentials 
committee; report of the commit- 
tee. 



4. Reading of letter of the pres- 
ident ot the party on the proposed 
coalition. 

5. Appointment of a platform 
committee. 

6. Nomination of candidates for 
president and vice-president of the 
Commonwealth; vote on the nomi- 
nations; appointment of committee 
to inform the nominees. 

7. Appointment of national cam- 
paign committee to work for the 
election of the nominees for pres- 
ident and vice-president. 

8. Other matters and resolu- 
tions. 

9. Adjournment.

ging Washington na ng Amerika, gayong si Washington ay

kapangyarihan.

ELECTION BILL DELAYED 

The election bill has encountered 
opposition in the House today which 
will delay its approval. There are 
many controversial points in the. 
proposed measure which are oppos 
ec 1 by many legislators, according 
to Representative Ramon Diokno, 
chairman of the House committed 
on rules. Among these points are 
th distribution of election in spec - 1 
tors, the grantnig of suffrage >o 1 
ron-Christian people, the divisoin 
of Mountain Province into repre- 
sentative districts and some miner 
points. 

It is believed that the measure 
T7 iH be T Assed by the legislature 
with many radical amendments. It 
is indicate! that, there will be' 
strong opposition in the upper 
house.

GenUomen of tho Convention. 
I " e government we are about to 
[establish under the Constitution, 
which our people have approved, 
save only in certain specified cases 
provided in the Independence Act, 
win have full power and responsi- 
bility to guide the destinies of our 
nation. Wo are beset from every, 
quarter and at every point by dan- 
gers and serious difficulties, and 
Tried men are required to overcome 



argument. Senate President Ma-

of the Nacionalist-Democrata par- 
ty yesterday in the Rizal Stadium, 
planted the issue of coalition 
which the convention, after pro- 
per discussion, enthusiastically 
adopted. 

President Quezon spoke more or 
/ess as follows: 

On behalf of the National Com- 
Siittee as well as on my own, 1 
bid you a heartfelt welcome. We 
gathered here to perform two 
of the most important functions of 
a political party: to approve a 

platform and to nominate candi- 
dates for the highest executive 
offices of the land.

and after President Wilson had

later. Moreover, powerful elements 
in America, both political and fi- 
nancial, were opposed to the grant- 
ing of independence and they did 
everything in their power to in- 
duce the American people to adopt 
the policy of permanent retention 
of the Philippines. 

^ was in the midst of such try- 
ing circumstances that our people 
through their chosen representa- 
tives and the instrumentally of po- 
litical parties undertook and car- 
ried on the campaign for the re- 
cognition of our right to become

pie in their epic struggle for eman- 
cipation. 

After a period of confusion fol- 
lowing the defeat of our armed 
forces* the Filipino people with a 
sew leadership undertook to ac- 
complish through constitutional 
processes what they failed to win 
in war. We had to pick up and 
gather the shattered fragments of 
the national life; the country had 
to be reconstructed from the very 
bottom, and the campaign for inde- 
pendence revived and taken up 
from where it had been left. 

President McKinley at the very 
outset of the American regime in 
the Philippines announced to {he 
Filipino people that the United 
States did not assume sovereignty 
over the Philippine Islands for her 
national aggrandizement nor from 
selfish motives, but only to pro- 
mote the welfare of the people of 
the Philippine Islands and secure 
for them the blessings of freedom 
and democracy 

In early declarations Presidents 
McKinley, Roosevelt and Taft as- 
serted that the process of gradual- 
ly placing in the hands of the Fi- 
lipinos the responsibility for gov- 
erning the Islands would eventual- 
ly result in independence, but they 
Baade it clear that a long and dif- 
ficult training would be required, 
lasting several generations before 
the Filipino people would be in a 



[ our part to brush aside every ob- 
stacle on the way; it demanded a _ 

rea]izati °n of the responsi-JJ deliberation, on the proposed "co^T 
bihties that we assumed with every tion of the two major political 



born wbei^the whole world is al 
most in turmoil, when grave so- 
cial. economic and political pro- 
blems are challenging the minds 
and hearts of men. and the very 

foundation* of civilized society j 
seem to be giving way. In this 
critical period the country needs 1 
all the wisdom, slatomanship and 
patriotism of which our people arc 
capable, and the men who are to 
be entrusted with the responsibi- 
lity of leading the nation through 
out of its present difficulties 
should be chosen, regardless of 
any other consideration, except 
ability .honesty and loyalty to the 
people and to the cause of goo r 
government 

It is with this end In view that 
when a group of Filipino citizens 
addressed me a letter on February 
fl®» 1035 > suggesting that a coalition 
,of the two major political parties 
be made for the selection of tho 
candidates for the Presidency and 
Vice-presidency of the Common- 
wealth, I gave it my unqualifie 
indorsement and approval. 

I shall ask tho Secretary to 
read the letter which I received 
and my answer thereto, (letter was 
read) 

Such was my view, after careful 



forward step in the conquest of 
our national rights to insure an 
honest, efficient and patriotic dis- 
charge of the governmental powers 
which from time to time were en- 
trusted to us. 

And so with .unflinching devo- 



parties on February 16, 1035. No 
thing that has happened since that 
time has caused me to change 
that view in the least Quite the 
contrary. Recent -occurences in 
America and all over the world 
including the Far East, as well an

tion to the cause which they em- in our own country, have more

position to decide for themselves 
■whether it were to their best inte- 
rest to become independent. In 
fact, as late ag the year 1022, upon 



braced, in the short span of a ge 
neratlon— not a long 'time, indeed, 
in the life of nations — from a state 
of defeat and complete subjection, 
without recognized political rights, 
through the untiring efforts of 
their chosen representatives, our 
people are emerging as a nation 
whose right to self-determination 
has been solemnly acknowledged 
by the American people. We exer- 
cised this right in the recent ple- 
biscite on the Constitution of the 
Philippines, and the decision of > 
: our people to became a self-govem- j 
ins, independent nation i3 already! 
an accomplished fact. 

In the near future there shall be 
occasion to recount in detail the 
achievements of cmr people during 
these thirty odd years in the task 
of nation-building. It is a record 
of which every Filipino may justly 
feel proud, for even the most pre- 
judiced foreign observers have fi- 
nally come to admit that it is un- 
paralleled in the history of subject 
peoples. At this time I shall only 
point to the fact that we have won 
our fight for independence and 
that its advent is assured. 



than ever convinced me of the. ne- 
cessity of marshalling all the re- 
sources of the nation, both human 
and material, to meet the gigantic 
ta3k before us. For this reason, T 
earnestly urge the convention to 
approve my letter which has iust_ 
been read, and in accordance” 
therewith, that we agree with tho 
Pro-Independencia Party to the j 
formation of one national ticket 
for the offices of President and 
Vice-President of the Common- 
wealth and to adopt a common pro- 
gram of government which will be 
submitted jointly by both parties 
at the coming general elections to 
the people of the Philippine Is- 
lands. . /

It is with this end In view that 
when a group of FJlioino citizens 
addressed me a letter on February 
10, 1935, suggesting that a coalition 
of the two major political parties 
be made for the selection of t.he 
candidates for the Presidency and 
Vice-preSidency of the * Common- 
wealth, I gave it my unqualified 
Indorsement and approval. 



I shall ask the Secretary to 
read the letter which I received 
and my answer (thereto, (lottera 
were read) 

Such was my view, after careful 
deliberation, on the proposed coali 



a * ♦iv M*«xi 










r '*v i-r. (.on 

ijtn. 

1 ° r the two major political 
parties on February 16, 1935. No- 
tning that has happened since that, 
time has caused me to change 
that view in the least. Quite the 
contrary. Recent occurrences in. 
America and all over the world 
including* the Far East, as well as 
in our own country, have more 
than ever convinced me of the ne- 
cessity of marshalling* all the re- 
sources of the nation, both human 
and material, to meet the gigantic 
task before us. For this reason, I 
earnestly urge the convention to 
approve my letter which has just 
been read, and in accordance 
therewith, that we agree with tha 
Pro-Independencia Party to the 
formation of one national ticket 
for the offices of President and 
Vice-President of the Common- 
wealth a,nd to adopt a common pro- 
gram of government which will be 
submitted jointly by both parties 
at the coming general elections to 
the people of the. Philippine Is- 
lands. 



I will now conclude, but before 
| I do so, I wish to express my 
j thanks in the name of the national 
committee to you all for having 
attended the convention. Those 
who have come from Mindanao, 
the Visayas, the Bicol region and 
the northern provinces of Luzon, 
whose coming, to this capital dur- 
ing this financial crisis has meant 
a big sacrifice for them, are espe- 
cially deserving of this acknow- 
ledgement. 

For my part, I wish to express 
to youL also my most profound gra- 
titude for the cooperation and as- 
sistance which you- have given me 
as president of our party. What- 
ever fortune the future may have 
foi' me, /I will always feel in this 
loyal cooperation of my fellow par- 
tymen a reason for gratitude and 
consolation, and remember always, 
with satisfaction and pride, the 
years that I have spent with you. 
In presenting the letter which I 
have written and in asking for 
your approval, I do not overlook 
the fact that many of my fellow 
partymen are opposed to the coa- 
lition, and, according to my infor- 
mation, among them figure many 
who arc among the most loyal and 
attached to me. I do not find in 
their attitude, however, any indi- 
cation that their opposition to the 
coalition means disloyalty to my 
policy nor a desire to put obstacles 
on the way to policies I might 
adopt for the party; on the contra- 
ry, I see in their attitude a proof 
of their confidence in me, that 
they know I not only disapprove 
of the opposition which my fellow 
partymen and co-workers offer, 
against any policy that I can "pro- 
pose, when that opposition is sin- 
cere, but also invite a frank ex- 
pression of opinion from all those 
with whom I share the res- 
ponsibilities before the country f6r 
whatever compromises the party 
may have entered into. But this 
I say to them, as well as to -my 
other fellow partymen, that if 
there has been any occasion in 
which I need your cooperation and 
your confidence' more, it is the 
present. I am not appealing to 
you in the name of the party, much 
less for my political and personal 

interests; I would be now the hap-

Special to the TRIBUNE 
Cebu, June 13. — The people of 
Cebu are ready to welcome Senate 
President Quezon and Senator Os- 
inena, who are bringing to Bohol 
the remains of the late Senator 
Jose A. Ciarin. The party is 1 due to : 
arrive here on Friday; on which .1 
day services will be held and high 1 
mass will be said by Archbishop 
Reyes at the Cathedral. 

The body will be transferred to 
the s.s. Mactcin for Bohol. 

The return to Cebu of President 
Quezon from Bohol next Saturday, 
will be featured with a fluvial pa- 
rade in the Cebu harbor and a pa- 
rade in Cebu, ending at Plaza ^Li- 
berty where speeches will be 'de- 
livered. Among the • speaker* will 
be Governor Sotcro Cabahug and 
Paulino Gullas, A banquet will 

be held at the home of the gov- 
ernor. 

Governor Cabahug requested that 
all houses in Cebu be decorated 
with flags. Big delegations of mu- 
nicipal officials are expected.

“ -r ° bVi0US that the affairs of > the capital city of

~ central government logicaUy 
5 exercise direct control over its administration and 

Manila is really the nerve-center of toe 
national body, a,nd nothing less than the Stats itself 
should direct its destinies. 

The question of security is paramount. The nation’s 
capital must be invulnerable to the danger of domestic 
assault,., civil commotion, and coups d’etat. The police 
force of the entire metropolitan area must be under uni- 
h r edj?ontrol. The national defense forces must have their 
headquarters within this same area. 

We are not anticipating an impossible turn of events 
when we point out the importance of security for the 
national capital. The occurencei of May 1st and 2nd 
are still fresh in our memories. Those occurences clear- 
ly showed that it was only the accident of poor strategy 
and leadership that prevented an effort to capture Ma- 
nila. * 

Now, a successful raid on this city is probably a re- i 
mote possibility, but that is no argument Why a new na- i 
tion should forget its safety entirely and take a chance 1 
of allowing its capital city to become, even temporarily 
the scene of any violent disturbance. 

There is but one means of minimising this risk, and 
that is, for the national government to maintain direct 
control over the affairs of its capital city, and extend its /

Mali kaya ang ganyang palagay? 

Hindi kaya iyan ang tunay na nasa isipan at loob nina 
Quezon at Osmena at ng madlang alagad nila? 

Tila mahirap na itanggi ang ganyang katotobanan. 

Kaya, susog diyan, ay maaaring sabihing may landasin 
pa rin ang iba upang lumakad ng ayon sa kanilang ibig. 

Nakabukas pa rin nga ang landasin. 

At makapagpapa.salamat ang lahat pagks’t hindi isinum- 
pa, -ni hindi minasama man lamang, bagkus ay binuksan pa 
nga at itinuro sa madlang may ibig, ang landasin ng panana- 
lungat. 

Nguni’t may panahon pa kaya, upang ang mga hindi ma- 
■kaamin kina Quezon at Osmena, ay makapagbuu ng isang la- 
piang maaaring magkaroon ng lakas upang makagawa ng isang 
katamtaman man lamang na panalungat?

lag na alagad nila, at walang salang magtatamo ng ganitong 
tugon :

rili — habang may lakas kami at buhay.

The proclamation of Senate Pres- 
ident Manuel Quezon and Senator 
Sorgio Osmena as coalition candi- 
dates for president and vicc-presi- j 
dont r respectively, of the common- j 
wealth, was tentatively fixed last I 
night for Saturday, July 0. 

An elaborate program lias been ! 
prepared by Bopxesentativc Itamon i 
Diokjio. Among its principal fca- j 
tures are a mammoth meeting on > 
the Now Lunetft where the procla- j 
mation will be made, the coming of | 
large delegations from the pro- j 
vinces, inarching of 100 bands 5 
around the city, meetings and fire- S 
works in the evening in Tondo and I 
Sampaloc. 

Big Crowd Expected 

It is expected that a crowd of 
at least 100,000 will gather at tho 
New Luneta to witness the procla- 
mation ceremony. Committees will 
• be appointed to secure .substantial 
reductions in transportation fares 
to permit as many people as may 
desire to come, to make this trip; \ 
to look after their welfare during > 
the sojourn in Manila; and other- I 
wise provide food and quarters for | 
them. 

Bo that absolute order and peaefe j 
may be maintained, the necessary j 
steps will bo taken, to prevent trou- j 
ble-inakers from doing anythin" ! 
that might mar' the proclamation. 
Law agents, including firemen with i 
their equipment, will be detailed tdj 
intervene at any moment and quell I 
all attempts by subversive elements ! 
to create trouble. No bullets will 
be used against them but there will 
i>e plenty of tear bombs and fire j 
hose to be brought into play, should 
the occasion warrant their use. 

To Be Marked 

All participants will be asked to 
wear a band across the chest. Pr 0 . 
vincial committees will see to it 
that the members of their delega- 
tions are provided each with a 
band, which will be made of hemp 
and will carry the national colors 
and the names of Quezon and Os-

Eager to Plunge Into Multitude of Affairs 
Leading to Establishment Commonwealth ~ 
Huge Congressional Delegation , Including 
Vice-President Garner and Speaker By ms, 
Coming for Inauguration~G. G . Optimis- 
tic Regarding Future Philippine Islands 



(By UDited Press) 

Manila, June 8 — The Dollar 
iner President Coolidge, bring- 
ng home Governor General 1 
J*rank Murphy and Senate Pres- 
cient Manuel Quezon from their 
successful and epoch marking 
nvasion of Washington, was 
locked at pier 7 this morning at 
line forty-five o’clock. 

A crowd of many thousands 
yere on hand to welcome the 
-eturning officials and gave them 
1 grand ovasiorv They were 
i riven through huge flower deck- 
ed arches of welcome near the 
legislative building and, were en- 
thusiastically cheered by the 
multitude. 

Governor Murphy continues to 
be very optimistic regarding the 
future of the Philippine Islands. 
However, he stressed the fact 
that economic relations with the 
United States must be equitably 

established. 

Murphy said that President 

Roosevelt is already prepared 



to call a Philippine trade con- 
ference in Washington immedi- 
atel,js^ifter the Philippine Com- 
monwealth is established. He 
said that it will also be necessary 
to win congressional support to 
a satisfactory program. 

The governor general also 
announced that a huge congres- 
sional delegation, including 
Vice-President Garner, Speaker 
Byrns, and some thirty odd 
congressmen, will probably come 
to Manila for the inauguration 
of the Commonwealth. Sec- 
retary of War George Demis 
also expected to come on the 
cruise. 

Senate President Quezon said 
this morning that he is eager to 
plunge into the multitude of 
affairs demanding his attention, 
including the special session of 
the legislature which has been 
called to convene June 12 for 
the canvassing of the plebiscite 
returns, and the majority party 
convention which will convene 
June 16.

Special to the TRIBUNE 

Cebu, Cebu, J.uue 21.— Cebu city 
will gTeet Senate Pre side nt Mami el 
and Party with an clabo- 
| rate reception on their arrival 
here on the Pa nay tomorrow 
; morning. 

Decorated launches will meet 
the boat off Mactan island and es- 
! cor £ ^ to the wharf. The recep- 
; tion committee will board the 
j Pa nay before it docks. 

At eight o’clock a land parade 
will start from the pier and march 
to Plaza Independencia where the 
senate president will plant a Que- 
zon tree, the planting ceremonies 
to be followed with speeches at th* 
bandstand. 

Besides President Quezon, Senat- 
or Sergio Osmeha and Cebu legis- 
lators will also speak. 

The period from ten to twelve 
o’clock has been set aside for con- 
ferences. At noon Governor Caba- 
hug will be host* to the party at 
luncheon in his place at Mabolo. 

At four in the afternoon Presi- 
dent Quezon will review constabu- 
lary troops and the cadets of the 
Cebu junior college and the pro- 
vincial high school at the normal 
school grounds. After the review, 
he will address a student gather- 
ing. 

Before the party leaves at mid- 
night for Bohol, they will be 
guests at a reception given by the 
Cebu community at the Club Fiv 
lipino. On his return here next 
Wednesday, President Quezon will 
again confer with political' lead- 
ers and municipal officials.

, Nueva Ecija, June IS. 
--Mrs. Aqrora Aragon de Quezon, 
distinguished wife of Senate- 
President Maquel Quezon, who was

mer Rep. and Mrs. FqJtoft.BUfllCy 
Jr., made an Impressive 
showing at the newly-constructed 
Cabiao Recreational Hall alleys 
last Saturday, June 15. 

Playing before a big crowd of 
local bowling enthusiasts, Mrs. 
Quezon, paired with Delegate Bo- 
nifacio Isip, scored a decisive vic- 
tory over Mrs. Felipe Buencamino, 
Jr. and her partner, Mr. Manuel 
Gonzales, in a game of three sets, 
the total scores of the winning 
pair being 523 pinfalls against 462 
of the defeated pair. 

In registering 262 pinfalls which 
wer e ithe highest individtal total 
scores of the tourney, Mrs. Quezon 
made one strike and three spares,’ 
to the surprise of many an admir- 
ing spectator when she was heard 
to say after the game: “I have 

pot played bowling for almost se- 
ven years 1 '.

To go or not to go. That was 
the paramount question in the 
minds of those/ composing the 
Quezon party as the hour sche- 
duled for the departure of the 
s. $. Bohol last night parsed and 
no senate president was forth- 
coming. 

Finally, the leader appeared 
and those who were to go with 
him heaved sighs of relief, hut 
were electrified into instant ac- 
tion the next moment when Mr. 
Quezon announced that he had 
postponed his departure. There 
was a mad, hurried scramble 
for bags and luggage and an 
exciting race down the gang- 
plank. 

Two newspapermen assigned 
to accompany the president loi- 
tered on the dock until the Bo- 
hol had sailed out of sight. “He 
might change Ms mind again , : " 
one of them remarked.

As American Editors See Us 

—REPRINTED EDITORIALS— 



It Looks 
Like 

An Error 



T H 1 It T Y- 
FOUR of the 
thirty-five pro- 
vincial governor:* 
of the Philip- 
pines have sign- 
ed a resolution 
endorsing Ma- 
nuel Quezon for president and Ser- 
gio Osmeha^bis principal political 
opponent, for vice president of the 
commonwealth to be set up in the 
islands v/ithin a year. It is be- 
lieved their selection represents the 
views of the island leaders, but in 
the event of their being elected 
a condition may be brought about 
precisely similar to that which 
existed in the earlier years of the 
United States. 

When John Adarns became the 
second president of the United 
States his principal opponent was 
Thomas Jefferson, the latter re- 
ceiving sixty-eight electoral votes 
to seventy-one for Adams, and 
under the constitution as it then 



stood becoming vice president. In 
the next presidential election, both 
Jefferson and Aaron Burr receiv- 
ed sevnty-three votes in the elec- 
toral college, throwing the selection 
into the house of representatives 
Jefferson was elected president 
*.nd, undej the rule, Burr became 
vice president. By the twelfth' 
amendment thereafter votes were 
taken separately for president and 
vice president, who thus became 
of the same party. 

The effect of the different view- 
of the president and the next hhdn 
cst officer was seen so plainly^, 
the one term of John Adams where 
Jefferson led the attack on the 
alien and sedition laws and on 
the president's foreign policies and 
again by the dissension between 
Jefferson as president and BuV 
as vice president, that a change 
in the Jaw became imperative It 
would seem that the islands should 
take heed of this experience of the 
republic to which they are about 
to owe their independence.

By MONTANO D. NAZARIO 
DMHM Staff Correspondent 
CEBU, June 26. — As the 
cutter Banahaw was steam- 
ing into Cebu harbor today, 
Senate. President Quezon gave 
out his views on the present 
right of the women to regain 
the ballot removed from them 
by the election bill, his words 

as he spoke almost drowned out 
by the cheers with which a big 
welcoming crowd greeted him 
upon arrival. 

President Quezon, commenting 
; on the women's fight said, “I 
j hope the women will bring the 
i suffrage case- to the courts, al- 
; though I think they cannot win. I 
1 am afraid that even the Legisla- 
ture cannot grant them the right 
to vote in the Commonwealth elec- 
tions because the constitution, in 
article V, provides that they can 
vote only after a plebiscite on 
suffrage has been held, and in 
which at least 300,000 of them 
choose to exercise the right to 
vote. 35 

The leader explained that under 
the Constitution this plebiscite 
may be ordered by the national 
assembly two years after the ap- 
proval of the constitution. 

“The Constitution is the supreme 
law of the land now,” President 
Quezon declared, thus clinching 
the argument against both the 
women who want to reclaim the 
ballot, and - those who argue that 
in cases of apparent conflict be- 
tween the Tydings-McDuffie law 
,and the Constitution, the former 
j should prevail,

purpose. He was vigorously ap- 
plauded before, during and after 
his speech. 

President Quezon, who received a 
great ovation, said that it is un- 
true that Senator Osmena sought 
the coalition, so as to regain his 
power. “I am the man mainly res- ; 
ponsible f<^r the coalition.*' he said. 

President Quezon then related 
how when, on board the s. s. Cool -

candidacies of Aglipay'and Agui- 
naldo, he told Osmena to reconsi- 
der his acceptance of the coalition, 
as in a four-cornered fight, he t,Os- 
meha) had a good chance to come 
‘out elected president. 

Senator Osmena replied: “Don't 

think further of it ” 

President Quezon, continuing, 
said that it is not true he wants 
the coalition because* he is afraid 
of the fight or because he Is afraid 
to assume full responsibility for the 
commonwealth. He said that he 
wants the coalition because this is 
not the time for a quavrel but ra- 
ther for cooperation. 

“If I am elected,” President Que- 
zon further said, “I will be a presid- ,

organization of "political parties is 
to serye the people. 51

It is good that President 
Quezon has declared that 
the government should not 
withdraw its hacking from 
all business in which it is at 
present engaged. And when 
he speaks of the present gov- 
ernment he must also neces- 
sarly refer to that of the 
commonwealth. This is a 
guide to the plans which 
should predominate during 
the coming government, 
keeping such policy for a 
country who is just starting 
life alone. 

It is true that in more ad- 
vanced countries, this policy 
is not always recommend- 
able. It may be like main- 
taining capital under an un- 
just guardianship. But in a 
country like ours where we 
are just beginning to orga- 
nize the government should 
be the true leader, in politics 
as well as in the develop- 
ment of business, in the or- 
ganization of industries, in 
the exploitation of natural 
resources and in the safe- 
guard of national wealth. In 
word, the government 



a 



should be the link, uniting all 
the factors of our national 
life until private iniciative 
gets sufficient strength to 
cope these undertakings 
without the aid of the gov- 
ernment.

Quezon T ells Cebu People He 7 | 

ought Coalition with Minority 




Tlle pronouncement of Mr. 
Quezon on the propriety of the 
government's staying in business 
has revived a 
question in the 
mind of curious 

mwir tm They 

| recall the pre- 

r _ cipitate fashion 
y**. , in which the 

\ a * government ice 

| f plant was made 

. \ to pass into prl- 

\ rate hands to 

the prejudice of 
certain infant 
Industries obviously in need of 
direct government encourage- 
ment. 

It might be explained that ice 
manufacturing in this country 
had reached a stage of progress 
that was best left to private 
initiative to exploit on a profit- 
able basis* Tbe government had 
done its part in bringing it to a 
certain point of development be- 
yond which it was plainly un- 
necessary to exert any further 
enterprise. The fact that the 
government ice plant was taken 
over by special interests espe- 
cially friendly and useful in other 
ways to the government leaders 
was quite accidental. 

It has been brought out that 
industries involving the preserva- 
tion of fish, meat, vegetable, fruit 
and such comestibles have regis- 
tered higher rates since the gov- 
ernment ice plant passed into 
private direction. It has also 
been brought out that such in- 
creased rates must of necessity 
be borne to the ultimate prejudice 
not only of the promoters of such 
young industries but of the con- 
suming public. 

All of which, we suspect, could 
be neither here nor there. The 
main issue was whether the gov- 
ernment should operate the ice 
plant further to the prejudice of 
its biggest private competitor. 
The concern was not whether any* 
smaller industries dependent 
upon the government ice plant 
would be adversely affected. 
The logical and only answer of 
course to the main issue was that 
the government • had no further 
business to compete. 

That the young industries con- 
sequently left to shift for them- 
selves did not count with plead- 
ers of prestige was no fault of 
the government leaders primarily 
responsible tor the complete with- 
drawal of the government from 
the ice business. The stars were 

simply not on thelr Slde * ,n 
other words, it was not In such 
small and young industries to be 
so lucky. 

But we are with Mr. Quezon in 
ihe jdea that the government 
should stay in business as long 
* conditions warrant. The gov- 
ernment can still go a long way 
® exerting enterprise in this di- 
in Ho n . Private initiative here 
r ® * perforce be timid for the 
011 t part by reason of the lack 
It J»° tradition of substantial achie- 
vement and success.

PA) Vein's June 1915 

The hot spell that for the last 
three days had settled on Manila 
continued unabated with the mer- 
cury sticking to the 32 degrees 

centigrade mark... With the 

Philippine assembly still enjoying 
its protracted furlough, naturally 
nothing was recorded of that body. 
..... Speaker Osmoha and Resi- 
dent Commissioner Manuel Que- 
zon, in company with the mem- 
bers of the congressional mission, 



’were touring the Visayas, but re- 
cords fail to show in what place 
* they were this day Gov- 

ernor General Harrison was in 
town but did nothing, officially or 

privately, worth chronicling 

The annals however noted the se- 
cond trip of Commissioner Jaime 
de Veyra to Tagaytay Ridge ac- 
companied by Architect Fenhagen, 
Engineer Kat.igbak, Dr. Antonio 
Sison and District Engineer Ben- 
nett in connection with his plan to t 
make that place a regularly-orga 
nizecl town and a health resort. 

Arriving the following day! 

Don Jaime waxed eloquent over 
the prospects of Tagaytay: “i am 
now more than ever fully convinc- 
ed an <i disposed to favor the ex- 
tension of the road to Tagaytay 
and to making that place a regular 
town. The splendid agricultural 
possibilities of ita rich lands would 
be the best justification.’'

RETAIN ENGLISH 

Will Be Language of Instruc- 
tion Above Fourth 
Grade 

The use of local dialects as me- 
dium of' instruction in the first 
four grades of th e primary schools, 
with English to be taught as one 
subject, will be recommended by 
the Quezon educational survey 
committee, was decided at a 
general meeting at the University 

of the Philippines yesterday aft- 
ernoon. 

Besides this recommendation, 
the survey committee agreed on 
a second, to recommend the rais- 
ing* of the standards of the vari- 
ous professions. 

With regard to the recommen- 
| dation on local dialects the com- 
J mittee agreed that this plan should 
i first be conducted on a controlled 
experimentation basis, the results 
to determine whether the system 
is to be made general. 

English to Be Taught 

Under the proposed plan, dia- 
! loots will be used as instruction 
medium from Grade I to Grade 
IV, English to be taught as one 
subject. From th e fifth grade up. 
however, English will continue to 
toe the language of instruction. 

^ The decision to make use of dia- 
lects came as the result of re- 
searches by o n 0 of *ho 
fmittees, showing that 65 per 
-c£_, those who enter the primary; 
schools stop studying after thej 
fourth grade, only 35 per cent con^jf 
tinuing their studies into the in- i 
termediate rank. 

Benefit 65 Per Cent 
Because of this, it is’ pointed out, 
these 65 per cent will get more 
benefit out of being taught the 
dialects than English, for although 
they may stop studying the state 
may continue to give them citizeiu 
ship training through the dialects. 

The recommendation on the rais- 
ing of the standards of the pro- 
fessions will be made more spa- 
cific later. It is proposed under 
this recommendation that entrance 
requirements to the professions ba 
made more strict. Requirements 
for each profession will be outlin. 
q(X later. 

The committee, on the sugges- 
tion of Ur. Gabriel Mahalac, de- 
vled to create a sub -committee on 
c1 duca tion of the non-Christian FL 
f-oinos. Dr. Pedro Guiang, and 
"f Isabel Ferrer were appointed 

• official researchers.

NASAAN ANG MESANG BILOG? 

S A maIas . a y hindi na magiging totoo ang magandang balak 

’ na masdao f dit0 isang panayam sa me- 
sang bilog ang mga pangunahmg lider ng isa't isang kandida- 
to, upang mapag-usapan ang pagtalikod sa hamak at salaula- 
tunggahan. Papasok tayo sa bagong panahon, at matapat na 
magbago naman sana ng mga “sandata sa t\*nggalian”. 

Marami ang nangagalak sa harap ng gayong balita. 

• „ “7^ nahon na nga anilang — itabi ang mabahong putik 
ng pohtika upang mapalitan ng malini- - t,jV.; e nr - mar^neal 
na tunggalian. * 

Hindi nga minamasama ang tunggalian, 

Isa pa ngang bagay iyan, na tunay na karapat-dapat. 

Lalong-lalo na nga kung “sandatang pangmaginoo” ang ga- 
gamitin ng nagtutunggali. 

Upang- sa gaanyang paraan ay maparangalan man lamang 
ang bagong panahon. 

Nguni’t ang tunggaliang salaula, ang paggamit ng mga 
sandatang hamak, mga katakata at kasinungalingan, hamak 
at lipos ng kaalibughaan, ay iyan na ang kasumpa-sumpa at 
hindi dapat gawin. 

Ano ang ibubunga ng mga ganyang kasinungalingan? 

Tama bagang imatuwid, na ang lahat nang bagay ay maaa- 
ring sabihin, kung panahon ng halalan at kung pamomolitika 
lamang? 

Ang buktot at ang kasinungalingan ay hindi tama, kai- 
lan man. 

At yaong sinabi ni Quezon, o yaong inibig niyang sabihin. 
na wolang saysay ang kanyang mga ipinaratang kina Osmena. , 
pagka’t pawang paratang lamang panghalalan, ay hindi sapat ; 
na maging sanhi ng pananatili ng ganyang kagagawan. 

Bakit nga magsisinungaling? 

- Bakit magpaparatang ng kaalibughaan? 

May palagay nga kaming ang salaulang tunggalian ay hin- 
di na bagay ipa.nhik sa sarili o malasariling tahanan. 

Tayo ang higit kang'ino man ay lalong dapat na magpita- 
gan sa sariling tahanan. 

At kung matapat ang ating nais na makapanumbalik ang 
yumaong Kepublika ay wala nang ibang dap^t sakitin, ngayon 
pa man, kundi ang pagpapanumbalik ng dating kalinisan sa 
halalan. Sa panahon ng Republika natin, lalo sa mga unang 
panahong yaon ng Himagsikan, ay hindi kilala ang salamangka 
at dayaan. 

Ang dayaan at kasalaulaan ay sa mga huling panahon na 
lamang ng kasalukuyang pamahalaan. 

At wala siamang- ibang layon sa gina.wang pagtuturo ng 
ganyan, matangi sa magkaroon dito ng mga kaguluhang sukat 
ikapagkaroon ng mga imperialista ng isang matibay na matu- 
wid laban sa kawalang kaya natin sa pagpapalakad ng Demo- 
krasia. Nguni’t. sa kagandahang palad natin ay nangabigo 
ang mga sulsol at pakana. Ang katinuan ng bayan ay naka. 
paghatfi rin, sa lahat nang pagkakataon. At alang-alang Sa 
ganyang tagumpay— na tanging sarili ng bayan at lahing itoL" 
ay tila wala nang napakabuting gawin, na gaya ng paranga- 
lang lalo at padakilain ang ganyang tagumpay, sa pamamagi 
tan ng pagtalikod sa madlang kasalaulaan.

BY OBSERVER 




Wonders Whether Filipi- 
nos Can Defend From 
Foreign Aggressions 

REPORTS RECEIVED 

QUEZON FIRST PRESIDENT OF 
COMMONWEALTH, MCARTHUR 
TO DEVISE DEFENSE 










B»ii AagKetongin 
Ki»a larpliy, Quezon 



(By United Press) 
Washington, July 2, — Lyle Wil- 

-son, United Press staff correspond- 
ent, said that due^ to the most per- 
plexed military problem in the 
world today, he is wondering how 
the Filipinos can defend themselves 
in the event of foreign agrression 
when they become independent. 

Apparently, reliable reports 
coming from Manila say that If 
Senate President Manuel L. Quezon 
will be selected president of the 
Commonwealth, General Douglas 
McArthur, now chief-of-staff of the. 
United States army, will be offered 
a jojb for devising a national de-^ 
fense system. 

Naval authorities, however, be-j 
lieved that it would be difficult for 
the United States navy to defend 
the Philippines in case a Pacific war 
would happen in which America be 
involved, due to distance from 
home bases. However, naval ex- 
perts disagree whether a properly 
.equipped and armed Philippines 
could defend itself. 

It is the concensus of opinion 
everywhere that should Japan de- 
cide to dominate the Philippines af- 
ter an independence will he obtain-! 
ed, Great Britain would actively op , 
pose for military and commercial 
reasons. - 

The Philipipnes in the hands of, 
the Japanese would menace the 
British colonies in the south and the 
maval base at Singapore. Japan 
would r\oz only menace British col- 
onies but also Dutch East Indies 
which in turn would become a good 
market for the growing volume of 
Japanese manufactured goods.

Ang pangulong Quezon ay hihi- 
ngaii ng paliwanag kung ang mga 
anti sa Maynila ay maghaharap ng 
kanilang kandidato sa kapulungang 
pangbansa sa hilaga at timog ng 
siudad., alinsunod sa napagkasun- 
duan kagabi ng lupon sa Maynila 
ng mga Nasionalista at Demokra- 
tang pro sa isang' pulong ' na idi- 
naos sa tahanan ng naging kina 
tawang Gregorio Perfecto. 

Ang pulong kagabi ay riakatakda 
upang' pag-usapan ang araw ng 
kombension ng mga pro sa siudad, 
upang liirangin ang mga kandida 
to sa pagkakagawad ng kapulu 
ngang pangbansa sa hilaga at ti~ 
mog ng Maynila. Iminungkahi ng 
naging kinatawang Remigio na 
ipagpaliban na muna ang pagta 
ta-kda ng araw ng kombension at 
kinakailangang hingin muna sa pa 
ngulong Quezon ng lupon ng mga 
pro sa siudad kung ang mga anti 
ay hihirang ng kanilang kandidato. 

Ang bagay na it-o, ay inilahad ng 
naging - kinataw’ang Remigio, ayon 
sa paliwanag, sanhi sa isang pa 
kikipagkasunduan ng lupon ng mga 
pro sa siudad sa Pangulong Que- 
zon na nagsasaad na sapagka’t ang 
Maynila ay lumilitaw na pro nang 
nagdaang halalan, ang mga pro 
ang may karapatang magharap ng 
kanilang mga kandidato. 

Ibig na maliwanagan ng mga pro 
ang itutugon ng Pangulong Quezon, 
sapagka’t sa kasalukuyan ay kuma- 
kampanya na sa timog ang mga 
abogado Teofilo Mendoza at Fran 
cisco, Villanueva samantalang ang 
abogado Engracio Clemenna ay 
kumakampanya naman sa hilaga ng 
Maynila. 

Gayon din naman pinagkaisahan 
nila na isang lupon ang makipag- 
kita sa lupon pangbansa ng mga 
pro, at ito ay binubuo nina Dr. 
Agaton Cecilio, abogado Prudencio 
Remigio, Dr. Pedro Gil, Pangulong 
Manuel de la Fuente ng hunta mu- 
nisipal at Inigo Ed. Regalado.

MADRID, July 10. —Widespread 
interest ia tho progress of the 
Philippine islands towards inau- , 
guiation of the Commonwealth 1 
l:his fall is displayed throughout 
K-.uain, which still feels strongly 
the kinship for the islands grow- 
ing out of three centuries of oon- 
tact prior to the war of 1898. 

It is also realized that ‘these arc - 
critical £imes in the islands. 

* When the Spanish parliament 
recently approved a resolution 
congratulating the peojae of the 
Philippines on the outcome of the 
independence plebiscite, Foreign 
Minister Juarn Jose Rocha said 
thak the Philippines were exper- 
iencing the most critical moments 
of their history since they will be 
independent in a few years. 

He praised Senate President 
Manuel Quezon, emphasized 

the work of the Spanish govern- 
ment in strengthening the ties tie- 
tween Spain and the Philippines., 
was noteworthy that all of the 
leaders of the parliamentary min- 
orities announced their approval 
of the congratulatory resolution. 

Deputy Perez Dias of the Repu- 
blican Union party said: 

‘‘Republicans can be contented 
, with this resolution because they 
always defended the liberation of 
tho peoples oppressed by the mon- 
archy and its sltupid laws which 
forced these people to separate 
themselves from Spain. 1 ’ 

His statement caused a loud pro- 
test from the monarchist deputies, 
who had to be called to order by 
fche president of the Cortes.

included a message which will be 
read at tonight’s 7 banquet should 
Justice Melliza fad to appear.

and interpretation in political cir-

means the withdrawal of the

Justice Melliza’s departure was in 
response to a rush telegram from

family.

Melliza’s departure, and his name

jP’ros And Antis Do Not 
W?.nt To, Abide By 
Agreement 

Mniilla furnished an added prob- 
lem to the legislative leaders in the 
selection of candidates for the na- 
tional assembly when it became i 
known yesterday that neither pros 
nor antis cn both sides of the coali- 
tion want to abide by agreement j 
alleged to have been reached 
among the coalition leaders. The 
antis have made representations to 
Senate President Quezon seeking 
the calling of a contention of the 
majority tor select the party can- 
didate and the pros on the othdr 
hand want to force the minority 
leaders to settle candidacy dis- 
putes by means of a party con- 
vention. 

A majority convention is sched- 
uled to be held just as scon as the 
legislature adjourns its session, it 
was explained. Joaquin Yuseco, 
former member of the municipal 
board, and Senator Juan Nclasco 
so far are the only two aspirants 
seeking the party’s endorsement to 
their candidacy. They propose to 
run for the -seat in national as- 
sembly from the north district of 
Manila. 

In the south district, the ma- 
jority ‘party also have various as- 
pirants but no names have yet 
been mentioned. Fci* the minority 
party, the choice will be between 
Gregorio Perfecto and Pascual 
Santos, a member of the board, for 
the north district. For the south 1 
district the aspirants are Pruden- 
cio Remigio, Dr. Pedro Gil and 
Mariano Nablew 

A trinagular fight in the city 
for national assembly seats is as- 
sured in view of the entry of Gen- 
eral Emilio Aguinaldo in the pres- 
idential race. Engracio Clemena, 
a city attorney, will run under the 
banner of the Radical party. The 
Radical party is affiliated in the 
election fight with General Agui- 
naldo’s National Socialist party. 

The three groups propose to hold 
conventions this month for the sei- 
| lection of t heir^of ficial candidates.

Examples: "‘Misbranding of fa- 

brics by imitating the well-known j 
trade-mark or brand of another 
manufacturer.” “Examples of this 
unfair method are the stamping of 
the mark "101* on inferior drill 
cloth, and Madapolan* on low 
grade coco cloth, etc. 

“Misbranding of imported wheat 
flour by placing third class flour 
in sacks or containers of second 
class one, and second class flour 
in sacks of first class flour. 

“Mislabeling of ropes and twines 
by manufacturers of inferior class 
of rope, so as to make their pro- 
ducts appear as the products of 
the well-known manufacturers." 

GROUP IV.— “Various schemes 
to deceive or defraud the public:" 
Examples: “Sprinkling water on 
rice, causing grains to expand and 
increase, in weight. 

“Erasing the word ‘imported’ 
printed on the shells of Chinese 
eggs by soaking the same in vine- 
gar, and selling the eggs.as native. 

“Inserting a- piece of board 
about \6 inch in thickness at the 
bottom of the liter or ganta, thus 
reducing the quantity of grains 
measured.”

procuring good s at the same terms 
accorded the conspirators, thus 
underselling their competitors. 

“Conspiracy between wholesalers 
and certain class of * retailers. If 
the supply of certain kinds of mer- 
chandise is almost exhausted, the 
wholesalers refuse to sell any to, 
other retailers but reserve the 
same to their co-conspirators, thus 
damaging the business of their 
competitors.”

Stresses 









Faithful Service 

Every Employe Responsible 
In New Government 



Senate President Manuel Quezon 
yesterday told the field agents of 
the buroau of commerce, now in 
convention in Manila, that a largo 
part of the success of the common- 
wealth government would lie In tho 
faithful fulfillment by government 
officials and employes of their 
respective duties. 

“You must realize,” he said, “tho 
responsibilities of every govern- 
ment employe in making tho now 
government a success. The com- 
monwealth government will not 
merely be a change of government 
officials, but the transfer of tho 
reins of the present government, to 
the Filipinos. AVo must see to it 
that the transfer is for the better.”

Democrats Hold Convention, 
Support Senate President 

The Democrata party which 
triumphed in the last elections in 
Cavite, home province of General 
Emilio Aguinaldo, at its convention 
in the provincial capital yesterday 
endorsed unanimously the candi- 
dacy of Senate President Manuel 
li. Quezon for commonwealth pres- 
ident and at the same time <nom- 
inated Attorney Angel H. Mojica 
as. its candidate for the national 
assembly in the com*"? general 
elections, 

This was reported to the . coali- 
tion leaders this noon by Rep. 
Francisco Area, president of the 
Democrata party in Cavite, who in 
a spirit of sacrifice declined to be 
nominated for re-election to the 
assembly in order to allow Attor- 
ney Mojica to be the candidate for 
assembly. All the nine Democrata 
presidents of the different munici- 
palities of Cavite and all the pre- 1 
sidents of the local committees of 
the party in municipalities where j 
there are Nacionalista presidents \ 
Were present at the convention and : 
voted for the candidacy of the se~ i 
nate president. 

Former Senator Emiliano T. Ti- 
rona and Delegate Demetrio En- 
carnacion attempted to hold a se- 
parate convention of the minority 
party also in the capital of the 
province but only Municipal Pres- 
ident Quiamzon, Democrata of Si- 
lang, attended their convention and 
nominated Tirona as the candidate 
for a seat in the national assem- 
bly. General Aguinaldo was pre- 
sent in the Tirona convention 
where his candidacy for the com- 
monwealth presidency w as also in- 
dorsed 

The Tirona conventionists ap- * 
pointed a committee of three per- 
sons to make overtures to the Ar- • 
ca followers who formed the great 
majority of the Democratas in Ca- 
vite so as to have a joint conven- 
tion. The Tirona proposal was 
turned down and the Area conven- 
tion proceeded with. all the leading 
minority men of the province in 
attendance. Even President Ro- 
sendo E. Santos, of the provincial 
capital, where the senate presid- 
ent lost in the last elections, was 
n resent in the Area convention and 
voted for Senate President Quezon 
as candidate for' the commonwealth 
presidency. This is ' an indication 
that the senate head will win this 
time in the provincial capital of 
. Cavite. , -Sp 

With the Democrata party and 
the Nacionalista party behind the 
candidacy of the senate president 
Cavite province, its native son, 
a&ne ral Emilio Aguinaldo, who, ac- 
cording to these Democratas, “de- 

' es their respect but should not 
rUe ioined politics, 1 ” is bound to 
Tose even in his home province.

If Mr. Quezon were not an extraordinary politician, 
a, sagacious statesman, a wonderful mixer; if he were 
not such an engaging person with something like a 
atai fascination in the look of his deep-set eyes; if he 
cici not look so like a god that his presence compels 
among the men a deferential bow and among the wo- 
men a rapturous smile; if he were not so imperial, so 
cosmic, so unattainable — we would still support him for 
the president of the Commonwealth because he dances 
the tango swell and because all the women within the 
orbit of his influence sigh so helplessly for him. 



It is a little distressing to think of so gorgeous a 
palace as the Malacanang converted into either a bar- 
racks or a cathedral. Not that we dislike passages of 
arms or the soulfulness of religious ritual, but that we 
would like to see the spirit of the dance dominate the 
rhythm of our public affairs. 



There is little enough comfort in the thought that 
the coming years will be trying for all of us. Why may 
we not be solaced by the knowledge that through all the 
-difficulties of the transition there will be interludes of 
happy laughter and sweet music and rhythmic move- 
ment in between the inevitable insanities over national 
defense, education, economic protectionism and the 
tariff? S

MANILA, July 15. — Preparations 
fer an impressive participation in 
the program to be neld in connec-

candidacies of Senate President 
Quezon and Sen. Osmena for the 
presidency and vice presidency of 
the Commonwealth respectively, are. 
almost complete, it was ’.earned this’ 
morning in the coalition headquar- i 
ters.

pected to arrive beginning tomorrow 
from far off districts. Bu(ae.m| 
alone will send 100 trucks and 200* 
automobiles to represent thj prov-’ 
ince. Other nearby provinces have’ 
piomised the same or even greater 
participations; j 

The first big delegation will come 
from western Visayas according to 
reports received from Governor Ti | 
meteo Consing of Iloilo. The delega- 
tion will sail from Iloilo tomorrow i 
from that city according to the Jloilo 
executive who is heading the band 
This will be composed of political 
figures from Iloilo, Negros, Anti-' 
quo and Capiz. 

According to present plans and 
enthusiasm no less than 100,000 
people will take part in the parade 
which will start at the pew Bonifa-: 
eio monument, on Rizai- Avenue and 
proceed to the new Luneta whetv 
the program will be held.

Murphy, Quezon Reach Accord 
On. Measure After Con- 
ference 



7 AVfc* ‘ 

7 



Governor General Murphy and 
Senate President Quezon in a con- 
ference at Malacaiiang yesterday 
morning agreed on the elec- 
tion bill, which will by no means 
be vetoed by the chief executive, 
it was learned yesterday, though 
it is held possible that the bill may 
be amended. 

If, as expected, several slight 
changes are contemplated, the 
measure will be returned to the 
legislature for immediate • correc 

i tion. . 

There will be no delay m the 

election bill’s approval by the gov- 
ernor general, which should serve 
to dispel anxiety on the part of 
the can at es for the National As- 

sembly ho are reported delaying 
their registration on its ’account. 

Governor General Murphy has 
studied the bill for two weeks and 
has received opinions of the secre- 
tary of justice and the members of 
his staff . 

If there will be any changes in 
the bill, it is believed that they will 
effect the provisions on the vote of 

non-Christians.

Raymundo Melliza, in a letter re- 
ceived yesterday at the Aguinaldo 
headquarters, says that the com. 
ments made on his sudden depar- 
ture from Manila were all a prod- 
1 net of the imagination of the coali- 
tion men. 

k : Justice Melliza explains that he’ 
left Manila suddenly chiefly be- 
cause he learned that coalition 
leaders in Iloilo were telling the 

people that he went to Manila to 

make a formal refusal of his nomi- 

na tion for vice-president. He also 

Coalition Leaders Will r ledge said that he suspects the same 
— thing is true in Manila, when his 

departure from this city was inter J 
preted as his withdrawal from the 
race . [ 

“Just as the people of Manila in- 
terpret my sudden departure from 
that city as a withdrawal of my 
candidacy/’ Justice Melliza said 
in his letter, “the people of Iloilo 
are being made lo oeiieve by coal? 
tion men that I have gone to 
Manila to make a formal refusal of 
being General Aguinaldo’s run- 
ning mate. I want to reiterate my 
public statements that I am stn 
a candidate because I want to be o! 
help in saving our country which 
has been maliciously misled for the 
last five centuries/’

at the Aguinaldo headquarters yes- 
terday, has been forming Aguinal- 
do committees in the Visayas.

As a preliminary to the celebra- 
tion that will take place in this 
city next Saturday on the occasion 
of the official proclamation of Sen- 
ate President Quezon and Senator 
Osmeha as candidates for Presi- 
dent and Vice-President of the 
Commonwealth, the coalition head- 



quarters will hold a program next _ 

Thursday night, the principal fea- i Headquarters have been opened in 

, - J Iloilo and other big towns of that 

ture of which will be the oath of n / . 



allegiance to the coalition banner ! 
and emblem. 

The program will be held at the 
coalition headquarters, on the third 
floor of the Plaza Hotel, at 8:30 
o’clock Thursday night, July 18. 
The 488 committees in Manila were 
grouped into 33 divisions, each 
under a campaign manager with 
the designation of “general.” The 
33 division generals \yill be present- 
ed on the night of the program, 
and will take part in the flag cere- 
mony, and in taking the oath of 
allegiance. Rep. Ramon Diokno 
will be master of ceremonies. 

The picture of a group of people 
carrying a nipa house on their 
shoulder, which was published in 
; the Sunday Tribune last June 2, 
was adopted by the coalition as 
its emblem. Flags and banners 
bearing this picture will be distrib- 
uted on the day of the celebration. 

It is calculated at the coalition 
headquarters that the trucks com- 
ing to the city will stretch for about 
10 kilometers when lined up. ' or 
this reason, the parade will move 
as follows: the trucks will line up 
« n both sides of the Bonifacio 
drive, while the parade will march 
in vhe middle of the street. 

Ex-Senator Aquino will present 
<= mate President Quezon, who will 
for about an hour Senator 
Arranz will present Senator Osmc- 
na, who i 3 scheduled to speak five

General Aguinaldo may leave for 
Iloilo next week, being unable to 
leave earlier due to his ailment. 
The official proclamation of the 
Aguinaldo-Melliza candidacy will 
be made in Iloilu upon the genr 
eal’s arrival. 

Aguinaldo and ex-Senator Tirona 
held public meetings in the towns 
of Bigaa and Hagonoy, Bulacan, 
last Sunday. Speaking in Bigaa, 
Tirona explained the death of An- 
dres Bonifacio, -for which General 
Aguinaldo is blamed, saying that 
Bonifacio was executed following a 
decision of the council of war. “If 
General Aguinaldo was to blame,” 
Mr. Tirona said, “the congress of 
Malolos could have fixed the blame j 
on him. The fact that Aguinaldo 
was elected president of the repub- 
lic was a vindication of the gen- 
eral’s leadership.” 

Aglipay in Tayabas 
Independence that will make 
everybody happy was promised by 
Bishop Aglipay to a group of prom- 
inenfc residents of Sariaya, Tayabas, 
last Sunday. The bishop-candidate 
visited Sariaya upon the invitation 
of Engracio Valderrama, in whose 
house a baptismal party was also 

The bishop said that, should he 
be elected, two months after his 
inauguration he would effect great 
economy in the government and 
cut down the expensive and luxu- 
rious government that is being 
conducted at present.

lipav issued a statement criticising

FOE SAMONTE,^. 

BY OLVIDO CORONA' 

Iniuf, Cavite * 

Now that Senate Pre$i4^ Q ue ‘ 1 
zon\ has given" His sanction to the 
holding of a special election in 
Cavite to choose the successor of 
the late Governor Pedro F. Espi- 
ritu, it is time for the people of 
that province to weigh the quali- 
fications of the various aspirants. 

The action of the senate head 
in acquiescing to a special elec- 
tion in order to .settle through 
legal means the rivalry between 
the pros and antis in the province 
deserves the praise of all fair- 
minded citizens of Cavite. It 
proves the sense of justice of the 
senate president and shows -his 
willingness to submit for popular 
decision a matter which rightly 
belongs to the people to decide. 

Being an aspirant for the high- 
est office in the new regime, Pre- 
sident Quezon could-easily have 
worked for the appointment of 
one of his men to serve the un- 
: expired term of the late Governor 
jB^piritu.' Thus, \)c could;' have put' 
; in a man who, out .of loyalty, 
would exert his utmost to promote 



President Quezon’s candidacy in 
the home province of General^ 
Aguinaido. 

> So the decision now lies in the, 
hands of the people of Cavite. 
The. problem now is to choose a 

[man who , will direct- the affair ' 
of the provVice e^c'entlv and vi- 
gorously, who has the tact neces* 

S-try to nb.jj 

his administration, who has the 
loyalty of the people .for whom 
nc will govern. 

Such a man is the present 
‘ fog incumbent, Bamon Samonte, 
who assumed direction of the af- 
fairs of the province even while 
the v late Governor Espiritu was 
still living. Board Member, ' and 
now Acting Governor, Samonte is 
the logical occupant of the vacant 
post of provincial governor. His 
political record is hia best recom- 
mendation, and the manner in 
which he has managed the prov- 
incial government in a temporary 
capacity sincG he was -appointed 
acting governor is a tribute to 
♦ this ability ahd the .respect in 
which he is held by the people, 

His handling -of the Sakdal up- 
rising in Cavite* to mention but 
on® instance of his tactful admi- 
nistration, will remain for some- 
time as one the spectacular ach- 
ievements of any provincial gov- 
ernor in the Philippines. While 
t)!ood was shed in two other pro- , 
vinces on account of, the lack of 
tact and caution, the uprising in 
Cavite, thanks to the cool-headed 
ness of Mr. Samonte, was quelled 
without much trouble. 

The manner, furthermore, In 
which he has gone about his du- 
ties in trying to curb lawlessness 
and criminality in the province 
has been most exemplary. He 
should be ' gllowed to continue the 
work he has scarcely begun. 

On my fellow citizens from Ca- 
ite therefore, I urge the. election 
Acting Governor Samonte to 
°!rve the rest of the unexpired 
term of the late Governor Espi, 

rituo _ /

Sin que el tiempo logre enfriar en lo mas minimo el eutusiasmo 
popular, das de 85 bandas ds musica., 984 vehiculos y unos 25,000 mil 
delegados de la coalicion, llegaron a las once de esta manana, proceden- 
tes de nueve provinces de Luzon, para asistir a la grandiosa procla- 
dBcicn de las candidaturas del Fresidente Manuel L. Quezon y del 
Senador Osmena, que se veriflcara a las ses de esta tarde, en la 
Luneta, Hasta anoche 15,000 delegados aproximadamente ya habian 
iiegado a la ciudad, procedentes de las provincias del Norte y del 
Sur de Filipinas. La ciudad de Manila tendra la mas grande dele- 
gacion, compuesta de no menos de 50^000 personas y 40 bandas de 
musica y mas de 500 vehiculos. Todas las provincias de Filipinas es« 
mran representadas. 

•" Desde la semana pasada el cuartel general de la coalicion ha 
estadc reeibiendc informes sobre la participacion de las provincias 
en la proclamacion, En todo el dia de ayer, iba caudillos de las nueve 
provincias de Luzon que tendran mayor numero de delegados estu- 
vieron en el cuartel,, para arreglarse con el comite de organizacion. 
La pi*ovincia con la delegacion mas grande sera Rizal que, segun se 
informo ayer al cuartel, tiene preparados 400 camiones y 24 bandas 
de musica. 'Las otras provincias cercanas, cuyas delegaciones llega- 
ran esta manana' son: Laguna, Tayabas, Batangas, Cavite, Bulacan, 
Bataan, Nueva Ecija y Tarlac.

TREGUA A LA LUCHA PARTIDISTA 

“ Aun desde que se aprobo y se acepto la ley de Independencia, se 
ha producido en el pueblo un sentlmiento creciente de profunda in- 
quietud por los problemas. dificilps y responsabilidades pendientes que 
tendremos que confrontar despues de la inauguracion de la Mancomu- 
nid ad. Mientras la Asamblea Const! tuyente se hallaba en sesiones, 
alentada en parte por el espiritu de solidaridad nacionai desplegado 
por los miembl’Os de dicha institucion, Ja ansiedad por preparar al 
pais adecuAdamente a encarar estos problemas y responsabilidades ha 
quedado articulada en una demanda popular cada vez mayor la de 
que los partidos politicos dieran tregua a la lucha partidista, que los 
caudillos de las fuerzas vitales de la nacion se unieran en sus fuer- 
zas para el logro del objetivo comun, y que todo ei pais se decidie 
ra a apoyar lealmente a los' hombres a quienes se podria confiar el 
caudillaje nacionai, con el fin de que, a lo largo de todo el periodo 
del gobierno de la Mancomunidad, pudieran asentarse solida y firme- 
mente los cimientos de la futura Republica Filipina. Concejos Mu- 
nicipals asi como organizaciones civiles* sin excluir uniones obreraa, 
aprobaron resoluciones demandando ima x coalicion de los dos gran- 
des partidos politicos, como el medio mas practico de obtener esta

La Constitucion 

"Considero la Constitucion de 
Filipinas como la expresion de la 
soberania y de la voluntad absolu- 
ta. del pueblo filipino. Acatare sus 1 
disposiciones. Mantendre el prin- 
cipio democratico que informa las 
instituciones que ella establece. Es~ 

! toy opuesto a una dictadura. Sos- 

tengo que a ningun individuo que 
fcrea en una dictadura sc lo puede 
lain riesgo alguno encomendar las 
riendas del poder ejecutivo bajo 
nuestra Constitucidn. La domo- 
solo podra sobrevivir 3i los 
que se hallan en las alturas del 
poder creen en el derecho del pue- 
blo a gobernar y tiene fe en su ca- 
pacidad inlierente de decidir con 
acierto importantes euestiones pu- 
blicas. 

"Declarare inviplables y defende- 
re hasta el fin los derechos y liber- 
tades individuales. Saivaguardare 
la libertad de la palabra, la liber- 
tad de la prensa, y la igualdad de 
todos y de cada uno ante la ley, 
por pobre o ignorante quo sea. 
Asegurare para cada ciudadano de 
Filipinas, de Luzon a Mindanao y 
Sulu. el derecho a adorar a Bios, 
segun los dictados de su concien- 
oia.

Meanwhile, both General Emilio 
Agrunaldo, socialist candidate, and 
Bishop Gregorio Aglipay, republi- 
can, and their men, were either out 
of town or were purposely avoiding 
comments on the speeches. All ef- 
forts to get some Aguinaldo hench- 
men to defend the general from the 
attack let loose by Senator Osme- 
ha virtually accusing him and his 
associates in the revolution of de- 
serting the Philippine cause were 
in vain. 

President Quezon's speech, it 
was remarked, is unanswerable in 
that its policies of government are 
obviously wise and constructive. 
Furthermore, the candidates and 
political henchmen called upon to 
criticize have not presented a pro- 
gram of government but have con- 
fined themselves to fragmentary 
promises^ and unbridled criticism, 
thus finding themselves in a pre- 
carious position for further cri- 
ticism. 

Although most business men and 
independent citizens were hiding 
from the rain and storm and could 
not be contacted for comments, it 
was stated by those who were cor- 
nered in the restaurants and other 
places downtown that the finan- 
cial, currency and industrial por- 
tions of the Quezon program are 
so aromatically wise that they de- 
serve nothing but indorsement.

SOUND IDEAS 



-Manuel Quezon did a creditable and 
workmanlike job in his speech of ac- 
ceptance of the nomination for Pres- 
s ident on Saturday. He outlined an at- 
titude toward government and a pro- 
gi am of administration which wins 
support and invites respect. 

Mr. Quezon is genuinely conserva- 
tive without being in the least Reac- 
tionary, and at the same time shows a 
liberal idealism which stops short of 
being radical. He expresses himself 
simply and directly without the resort 
,to exaggeration for emphasis. 

Jt is one thing, however, to enun- 
ciate such a program and another to 
carry it out. The enunciation depends 
upon Quezon. Putting such a program 
into practice depends not only upon him 
but also upon his political colleagues, 
subordinates and assistants. 

In the long run, it goe^ even fur- 
ther, and depends, to a large extent 
upon the electorate as a whole. 



Yeai 

in and year out. any people o- e f w! 2 at 11 must translate into concrete action, J 
, , ' * y people & et jua the sum oi its attitudes, judgments and . 



becomes a public duty to give consistent and 1 
alert attention to the consummation of the 
ideals which he has outlined. 

It is worthy of particular attention that 
Mr Quezon, in accepting this position of 
ea eisnp makes very 'few campaign prom- 
ises. Instead of them he invites obligations 
and responsibilities. This should set a key, 
no foi the electorate. Good government 
W1 . c ^ e P e ndent 1 u^on what the people put 
111 0 it, 1 ather than what they seek to get 
out of it. 

i ; ^ as enthusiastically committed 

himselt to a government which is upright, 
economical sagacious, friendly and alert to 
the public need. He can head such a gov- 
ernment only if the people who put him in 
ollice leally want it. He cannot stand long 
on those principles if he is obliged to stand 
alone. He must have help. 

rp, type loyalty is now required. 

1 le {^ 1 | 1 l ;) l :)lnes has always been intensely 
oyal to the person and personality of Manuel 
Quezon. It must now go further and dem- 
onstrate a dynamic loyalty to his ideas. If; 
is a sink or swim proposition. 

Grave problems are being faced. Leader- 
ship has been developed and a program of 
meeting some of those problems outlined. 
Merely saving that a thing ought to be done, 
however, will not accomplish it. It is well 
to give voice to ideals and aspirations, to 
make plans and programs, but the solict busi- 
ness of government must go further than ; 
that. It must translate into concrete action. 



about as good a government as they 
deserve. If they are alert and vigilant 
their government will reflect those 
qualities. If they are apathetic or .un- 
mindful, they invite abuse. 

Manuel Quezon is known as a political 
wonder-worker. But not even the dynamism 



beliefs 

This can not be done by the mere process 
of wishful thinking. It ‘can only be done 
by co-operative action. 

If the Philippines is to profit by the good 
sense of Manuel Quezon and if it is to utilize 
this transition period to build up a govern- 
ment which is honest, capable, stable, solvent 
and progressive, its individual men must 
possess and reflect those qualities. 



of Quezon can convert the empty form of, bpssess and reflect those qualities.. Speci- 

e ,1 fically, if Quezon s government is to be 

government, however well reasoned, into the honest> individual members of the assembly. 

active reality of good and sensitive adminis- provincial governors, under-secretaries and 
tration unless ‘ft derives its power and its justices of the peace must be honest. If the 

• v/-. 4- 1 n 4- /x K \ 1 a ti I- 1 rn , . 1 1 1 ■ 1 / « 1* 



punch from vigorous and enlightened public 
support. 

Whether or not the electors back Mr. 
Quezon in the forthcoming political cam- 
paign is a matter which they must settle 
with their own consciences. After he is 
elected, (which seems extremely likely) it 






government is to be alert, its citizens must 
be vigilant. If it is to be solvent, its electors 
and their representatives must believe in 
solvency. 

Quezon wants to give the Philippines a 
good government. He will be able to do so 
to approximately the extent that the people, 
as a whole, concur in his desire.

SUPERSTITIOUS 



i ^ board the s.s. Luzon,, which 
the Quezon party chartered 
while returning to Manila 
irom Bohol where legislative lead- 
er s . attended the funeral of the late 
kep . Clarin, one incident occurred 
which many in the party t:;ke de- 
light in relating 

An under-secretary appeared in 
the breakfast room with if* puzzled 
look one morning. He had been 
surveying the breakfast table, and 
had counted the number of persons 
already seated. "Twelve” lie mur- 
mured and started to leave the room. 

Seeing him, Senate President 
Quezon, who -was already seated, 
asked: “Que.pasa?” 

Somebody near the Senate Pres- 
ident offered the explanation: there 
were already twelve persons around 
the table and another one would 
make the number thirteen. 

Hearing this, Quezon instantly 
flared: “Ay, caramba! What will 

that do you?” 

The under-secretary had to take 
a seat, much against his will.
